                                                   specific eGFR levels indicating renal impairment for this to be applied.
                                                
        • 
                                             
      
                                          
      • 
                                       
    
                                       
    Be aware that withholding metformin results in hyperglycemia.
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • If your patient is taking other glucose-lowering medication,
                                             these may need to be increased in dose or an alternative
                                                hypoglycaemic medication to metformin prescribed.
      • 
                                          
    • Some patients may need insulin as a temporary measure,
                                             but seek diabetes inpatient specialist team advice.
      • 
                                       
    
                                       
    Stop sodium-glucose cotransporter-2 (SGLT-2) inhibitors in all acutely ill
                                             patients, particularly if dehydrated or with infection, as they are at risk of
                                             euglycaemic ketoacidosis.
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • SGLT-2 inhibitors (e.g., dapagliflozin) reduce blood glucose
                                                reabsorption in the kidneys (independently of insulin metabolism of
                                             glucose). (Peters AL, et al, 2015) 
      • 
                                          
    • They can mask underlying ketoacidosis by patients having a
                                             normal (or near normal) serum glucose level (euglycaemic
                                                ketoacidosis).
      • 
                                          
    • Check blood ketones as urinary ketone measurement may be
                                             unreliable.
      • 
                                          
    • Treat for DKA if blood ketone level is ≥3
                                                mmol/L, blood pH <7.3 and bicarbonate
                                                <15 mmol/L. (JBDS-IP, 2013)
      • 
                                       
    
                                       
    Reduce or omit the dose of gliclazide if the patient has impaired kidney
                                             function or is eating less than usual, to avoid hypoglycaemia at night. 
                                       
    
                                       
    Inform the diabetic inpatient team of the admission of any patient with
                                             diabetes. They are available for advice and support.
    
                                       
    For a patient at the end of life: (Rowles S, et al 2011)
                                       
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • Hypoglycaemic medication should be reduced to
                                             the minimum, but
      • 
                                          
    • The patient should be kept free from symptomatic
                                                hyperglycaemia, which can lead to dehydration,
                                             ketosis or hyperosmolar hyperglycaemia.
      • 
                                       
    
                                       
    Refer to local protocols or the Association of British Clinical
                                          Diabetologists (ABCD). (Rowles S, et al 2011)
    
      
                                    
    
      
                                 
    
                                 
                              
    
                              
    
                                 
    
                                    
    
                                       
                                          
                                             
                                                
                                                   Plus
                                                    – 
                                                
                                                
                                                   
                                                   Monitor and manage blood glucose
                                                
                                                
                                                   
                                                
                                             
                                          
                                       
                                    
    
                                 
    
                                 
    
      
                                    
    
                                       
                                       
                                       
    
      
                                       
    Monitor blood glucose levels at least 4 times a day (pre-meal and before
                                             bedtime if eating) in all acutely unwell patients with diabetes mellitus
                                             (American Diabetes Association, 2019)
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • The risk of hypo- and hyperglycaemia increases when people with
                                             diabetes are admitted to hospital with acute conditions. (NHS Digital.
                                                National Diabetes Inpatient Audit (NaDIA) - 2017, 2018)
      • 
                                          
    • More frequent monitoring is needed after episodes of
                                                hyperglycaemia or hypoglycaemia.
      • 
                                          
    • More frequent monitoring is needed after a change in
                                                hypoglycaemic medication.
      • 
                                          
    • Patients on a variable rate intravenous insulin infusion
                                                (VRIII) need capillary blood glucose measurements every
                                                hour. (Joint British Diabetes Societies for inpatient care
                                                [JBDS-IP], 2014)
      • 
                                          
    • Support self-management (including monitoring blood glucose) if
                                             this conforms to local protocols, it is safe to do so, and the patient is
                                             willing. (Joint British Diabetes Societies for inpatient care [JBDS-IP],
                                                2012) 
      • 
                                       
    
                                       
    Prevent and manage hyperglycaemia if it occurs
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • There is no consensus on target blood glucose levels for people
                                             with diabetes in hospital.
                                             
        
                                                
      • The Joint British Diabetes Societies for Inpatient Care
                                                   (JBDS-IP) recommend an ideal range of between 6 to
                                                      10 mmol/L, and an acceptable range of
                                                   4 to 12 mmol/L(JBDS-IP, 2014)
        • 
                                                
      • The American Diabetes Association recommend a
                                                   target blood glucose range of 7.8 to 10.0
                                                      mmol/L (140-180 mg/dL) for most critically and noncritically
                                                   ill patients. (American Diabetes Association. Diabetes Care in the
                                                      Hospital: Standards of Medical Care in Diabetes—2019, 2019)
                                                
        • 
                                             
      
                                          
      • 
                                          
    • The National Institute for Health and Care Excellence
                                                recommends a target plasma glucose level of 5 to 8 mmol/L in
                                                adults with type 1 diabetes in hospital undergoing surgery or with acute
                                                illness. (NICE, 2017)
      • 
                                       
    
                                       
    More liberal blood glucose targets are appropriate for people
                                          at high risk of falls and who are frail or have
                                          dementia. (JBDS-IP, 2014)
    
      
      
                                       
    
                                          
    
                                             
                                             
                                             
    
                                                
    Hyperglycaemia in patients in hospital is associated with adverse
                                                      outcomes. (Moghissi, 2009)
    
                                                
      
                                                   
    • Hyperglycaemia in patients in hospital, whether with known diabetes or
                                                      not, has been shown by various observational studies to be associated
                                                      with adverse patient outcomes, including increased mortality.
                                                      (Moghissi, 2009)
      • 
                                                
    
                                             
    
                                          
    
                                       
    
      
                                       
    
                                          
    
                                             
                                             
                                             
    
                                                
    Conflicting evidence for optimal blood glucose targets has led to
                                                      variations in recommendations in different countries and settings.
                                                      Follow your local protocol.
    
                                                
      
                                                   
    • Intensive care setting
                                                      
        
                                                         
      • A randomised controlled trial (RCT) of critically ill patients in
                                                            a primarily surgical intensive care setting found lower patient
                                                            mortality with tight glucose control - 80 to 110 mg/dL (4.4 to 6.1
                                                            mmol/L) compared with ‘conventional’ more liberal
                                                            glucose control. (Van den Berghe G et al, 2001) 
        • 
                                                         
      • However, a subsequent multicentre RCT in critically ill medical
                                                            and surgical patients in other intensive care settings found
                                                            increased mortality with tighter glucose control, possibly due to
                                                            increased episodes of hypoglycaemia. (Finfer et al, 2009)
                                                         
        • 
                                                      
      
                                                   
      • 
                                                
    
                                                
    A 2010 systematic review of 6 RCTs and a meta-analysis investigating tight
                                                   glucose control (80 to 110 mg/dL [4.4 to 6.1 mmol/L]) vs less strict
                                                   glucose control in critically ill patients in the ICU setting found no
                                                   significant improvement in mortality with tight glucose control, but it was
                                                   associated with significantly more hypoglycaemic episodes compared with
                                                   less strict glucose control. (Marik, 2010)
    
                                             
    
                                          
    
                                       
    
      
                                       
    Follow your local hospital protocol on steps to take if your patient’s
                                             capillary blood glucose is ≥15 mmol/L. Exclude diabetic ketoacidosis (DKA) or
                                             hyperosmolar hyperglycemic state (HHS), both of which require specific urgent
                                             management.
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • Treat hyperglycaemia to avoid DKA and
                                                HHS.
      • 
                                          
    • DKA and HHS are medical
                                                emergencies. Follow JBDS or local hospital guidelines for patients
                                             with DKA (JBDS-IP, 2013) or HHS (JBDS-IP, 2012). 
      • 
                                          
    • The diabetes inpatient specialist team should be involved in
                                             the management of any patient with DKA. (JBDS-IP, 2013)
                                          
      • 
                                          
    • Early senior review by a clinician who is familiar with the
                                             management of HHS is imperative. (JBDS-IP, 2012)
                                             Patients with other comorbidities may need to be on a
                                             high dependency unit. (JBDS-IP, 2012)
      • 
                                       
    
                                       
    Be aware that the following medication may be associated with hyperglycaemia
                                             and may need to be reviewed: (Abdur R, et al, 2011)
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • Corticosteroids
      • 
                                          
    • Some beta-blockers (e.g., propranolol, atenolol)
      • 
                                          
    • Thiazide diuretics (e.g., hydrochlorozide)
      • 
                                          
    • Some second-generation antipsychotics (e.g., olanzapine and clozaine)
      • 
                                          
    • Certain fluoroquinolone antibiotics (e.g., gatifloxin)
      • 
                                          
    • Calcineurin inhibitors (e.g., cyclosporine, tacrolimus)
      • 
                                          
    • Protease inhibitors (e.g., as a component in antiretroviral therapy, such as
                                             ritonavir)
      • 
                                       
    
      
      
                                       
    
                                          
    
                                             
                                             
    
                                             
    Ask for expert advice from the diabetes team in complex patients, or
                                                   where hyperglycaemia is difficult to control.
    
                                          
    
                                       
    
      
                                       
    Prevent and manage hypoglycaemia if it occurs
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • Monitor blood glucose in people with diabetes in hospital and
                                             adjust medication in response to illness and hospital meal
                                             times, in order to reduce risk of hypoglycaemic
                                                episodes.
      • 
                                          
    • 1 in 5 inpatients with diabetes in England and Wales have a
                                                hypoglycaemic episode during their hospital stay. (NHS Digital.
                                                National Diabetes Inpatient Audit (NaDIA) - 2017, 2018)
      • 
                                          
    • Causes include: (JBDS-IP, 2018)
                                             
        
                                                
      • Recovery from an acute illness
        • 
                                                
      • Insulin or oral hypoglycaemic medication error
        • 
                                                
      • Wrong timing of insulin in relation to meals
        • 
                                                
      • Patients eating less but taking the same amount of diabetes medication
                                                
        • 
                                                
      • No bedtime snacks
        • 
                                                
      • Reduced appetite or vomiting.
        • 
                                             
      
                                          
      • 
                                       
    
      
      
                                       
    
                                          
    
                                             
                                             
    
                                             
    Bedtime snacks can reduce the risk of early morning hypoglycaemia.
                                                   (NHS Digital. National Diabetes Inpatient Audit (NaDIA) - 2017,
                                                   2018)
    
                                          
    
                                       
    
      
                                       
    Treat hypoglycaemia actively if the blood glucose falls below 4.0 mmol/L.
                                          (JBDS-IP, 2018)
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • Follow hospital protocol. The JBDS-IP has guidelines on hospital management of
                                             hypoglycaemia in adults with diabetes. (JBDS-IP, 2018)
      • 
                                       
    
      
                                    
    
      
                                 
    
                                 
                              
    
                              
    
                                 
    
                                    
    
                                       
                                          
                                             
                                                
                                                   Plus
                                                    – 
                                                
                                                
                                                   
                                                   Check baseline kidney function
                                                
                                                
                                                   
                                                
                                             
                                          
                                       
                                    
    
                                 
    
                                 
    
      
                                    
    
                                       
                                       
                                       
    
      
                                       
    Check baseline serum kidney function on admission in all patients with a
                                             previous history of chronic kidney disease (CKD). [National
                                             Institute for Health and Care Excellence. 2013)
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • CKD is a significant risk factor for the
                                             development of acute kidney injury. (Hsu CY, et al,
                                                2008)
      • 
                                          
    • Acute illness increases the risk of deterioration in renal function.
      • 
                                       
    
      
                                    
    
      
                                 
    
                                 
                              
    
                              
    
                                 
    
                                    
    
                                       
                                          
                                             
                                                
                                                   Plus
                                                    – 
                                                
                                                
                                                   
                                                   Baseline neurological assessment
                                                
                                                
                                                   
                                                
                                             
                                          
                                       
                                    
    
                                 
    
                                 
    
      
                                    
    
                                       
                                       
                                       
    
      
                                       
    Do a baseline neurological assessment on admission for all patients with a
                                             history of stroke.
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • Patients with acute illness (including infections and causes of
                                             hypotension) are at increased risk of stroke (both ischaemic and
                                             haemorrhagic). (Grau AJ, et al, 2010) (Eigenbrodt ML, et al, 2000)
      • 
                                          
    • If there is a suspicion of a change in neurological status during admission,
                                             repeat theneurological assessment in case of a possible
                                                new stroke.
      • 
                                          
    • Following assessment, consider appropriate level of patient
                                                supervision e.g., relating to possible confusion at
                                             night, falls related to frailty.
      • 
                                       
    
      
                                    
    
      
                                 
    
                                 
                              
    
                              
    
                                 
    
                                    
    
                                       
                                          
                                             
                                                
                                                   Plus
                                                    – 
                                                
                                                
                                                   
                                                   Baseline cognitive assessment and collateral
                                                      history
                                                
                                                
                                                   
                                                
                                             
                                          
                                       
                                    
    
                                 
    
                                 
    
      
                                    
    
                                       
                                       
                                       
    
      
                                       
    Do a baseline cognitive assessment in all older patients, particularly those
                                             with a history of dementia. (Pendlebury ST, et al, 2015)
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • Use a validated scoring system that is also feasible in the
                                             acute setting, such as:(Pendlebury ST, et al, 2015)
                                             
        
                                                
      • The abbreviated mental test score (AMTS/10)
                                                   (Hodkinson,1972)
        • 
                                                
      • Montreal Cognitive Assessment (MoCA) (Nasreddine,
                                                      2005).
        • 
                                             
      
                                          
      • 
                                       
    
                                       
    Dementia increases the risk of delirium. 
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • This can acutely reduce patients' cognitive assessment scores. (George J,
                                                2013)
      • 
                                       
    
                                       
    Take a collateral history from family, friend or carer, to establish the
                                             patient’s level of function and baseline cognition before this
                                             illness.
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • It’s important to know if the decline in function and
                                             cognition has been gradual or acute.
      • 
                                       
    
                                       
    Use this information for monitoring clinical improvement, and for
                                          establishing needs on discharge.
    
      
                                    
    
      
                                 
    
                                 
                              
    
                              
    
                                 
    
                                    
    
                                       
                                          
                                             
                                                
                                                   Plus
                                                    – 
                                                
                                                
                                                   
                                                   Assess for and manage delirium
                                                
                                                
                                                   
                                                
                                             
                                          
                                       
                                    
    
                                 
    
                                 
    
      
                                    
    
                                       
                                       
                                       
    
      
                                       
    Assess the patient with de mentia for delirium. (National
                                             Institute for Health and Clinical Excellence. 2010)
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • People living with dementia are at increased risk of delirium when they are
                                             admitted to hospital. (National Institute for Health and Clinical Excellence,
                                                2018)
      • 
                                          
    • Use a screening tool, such as:
      
                                             
        
                                                
      • The 4-AT(Bellelli, 2014)
        • 
                                                
      • The short confusion assessment method (S-CAM), as
                                                   recommended by the National Institute of Health and Care Excellence (NICE).
                                                   (National Institute for Health and Clinical Excellence. 2010)
        • 
                                                
      • Document the results clearly.
        • 
                                             
      
                                          
      • 
                                       
    
                                       
    Assess the patient for any reversible causes of delirium.
                                          (National Institute for Health and Clinical Excellence. 2010)These include:
                                       
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • Infection
      • 
                                          
    • Pain
      • 
                                          
    • Dehydration
      • 
                                          
    • Constipation
      • 
                                          
    • Hypoxia
      • 
                                          
    • Immobility
      • 
                                          
    • Medications
      • 
                                          
    • Poor sleep
      • 
                                          
    • Sensory impairment (e.g., ear wax or loss of glasses)
      • 
                                       
    
                                       
    Investigations to help diagnose underlying causes include:
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • Full blood count, electrolytes, renal function, liver function tests, calcium,
                                             glucose, CRP, folate and vitamin B12.
      • 
                                          
    • Blood cultures (if bacteraemia is suspected)
      • 
                                          
    • Urine microscopy and culture
      • 
                                          
    • Chest x-ray
      • 
                                       
    
                                       
    Manage according to the cause.
    
                                       
    Manage patients with delirium initially with non-pharmacological treatment.
                                          (National Institute for Health and Clinical Excellence. 2010)
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • Reduce disorientation by providing a well-lit
                                                room, with a clock and calendar
                                             visible (e.g., on the wall).
      • 
                                          
    • Encourage and facilitate family, friends and carers visiting
                                             the patient.
      • 
                                          
    • Use verbal and non-verbal techniques to de-escalate conflict
                                             and distress.
      • 
                                       
    
                                       
    Where non-pharmacological treatments are ineffective, and the patient is
                                             distressed or considered a risk to themselves or others, short-term antipsychotic
                                             or sedative drugs may be used.
    
                                       
    Only use antipsychotics as a last resort and not in patients with
                                             Parkinson’s disease or dementia with Lewy bodies.(National
                                             Institute for Health and Clinical Excellence. 2010)
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • Risperidone has the least anticholinergic effects. (Tracy
                                                JI, et al, 1998)
      • 
                                          
    • Haloperidol is also licenced for this indication, but has the
                                             highest risk associated with its use. The UK National Institute for Health and
                                             Care Excellence recommends short-term use of haloperidol (usually for less than
                                             one week), starting at the lowest dose and titrating
                                                carefully according to the symptoms. (National Institute for
                                                Health and Clinical Excellence. 2010)
      • 
                                          
    • Consider sedation in significantly agitated patients who pose a risk to
                                             themselves or others. Lorazepam is a suitable choice, but it
                                             should only be given orally or intramuscularly (i.e., not intravenously).
      • 
                                          
    • Lorazepam should only be used in the smallest possible dose for
                                             the shortest possible duration.
      • 
                                       
    
                                       
    Avoid typical antipsychotics, particularly haloperidol in patients with
                                             Parkinson’s disease or dementia with Lewy bodies. (National
                                             Institute for Health and Clinical Excellence. 2010)
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • The dopamine receptor antagonistic effect of typical antipsychotics can
                                             dramatically worsen physical and cognitive symptoms in people
                                             with Parkinson’s disease or with dementia with Lewy bodies.
      • 
                                          
    • Consider a low-dose short-acting oral benzodiazepine, such as
                                             lorazepam, or low-dose quetiapine in patients with
                                             Parkinson’s disease or dementia with Lewy bodies who require sedation to
                                             enable safe care because of delirium.
      • 
                                          
    • Consult your local drug formulary for advice.
      • 
                                       
    
                                       

    Document any episodes of current or resolved delirium in
                                          the discharge summary for the GP. (National Institute for
                                             Health and Clinical Excellence. 2010)
    
      
                                       
    
                                          
    
                                             
                                             
                                             
    
                                                
    Short-term antipsychotics may be needed in people with dementia to
                                                      enable safe care. However, antipsychotics have various adverse effects
                                                      in the elderly and are associated with an increased risk of death in
                                                      people with dementia.
    
                                                
      
                                                   
        
                                                      
      • A meta-analysis found that people with dementia who take atypical
                                                         antipsychotics have an increased mortality risk
                                                         compared with people taking placebo. (Ma H, et
                                                            al, 2014)
        • 
                                                   
      
                                                
    
                                                
      
                                                   
    • A large cohort study of elderly people found that higher
                                                            doses of antipsychotics are generally associated with
                                                         greater risk. (Huybrechts K, 2012)
                                                   
      • 
                                                
    
                                                
      
                                                   
    • It also found that of all the antipsychotics studied,
                                                      haloperidol has the highest risk associated with its
                                                      use. (Huybrechts K, 2012)
      • 
                                                
    
                                                
      
                                                   
    • Contrary to popular belief, long-term antipsychotic prescriptions in
                                                      people with dementia whose behaviour is now settled, can be
                                                      withdrawn safely in the majority of cases. (Van
                                                         Leeuwen, 2018)
      • 
                                                
    
                                             
    
                                          
    
                                       
    
                                       
    
                                          
    
                                             
                                             
                                             
    
                                                
    A non-randomised trial studying how to reduce risk factors for
                                                      delirium compared a multicomponent strategy with usual care in 852
                                                      elderly patients (over 70 years) in hospital. (Inouye,
                                                      1999)
    
                                                
      
                                                   
    • The multicomponent strategy targeted the following 6 risk factors:
        
                                                         
      • cognitive impairment
        • 
                                                         
      • sleep deprivation
        • 
                                                         
      • immobility
        • 
                                                         
      • visual impairment
        • 
                                                         
      • hearing impairment
        • 
                                                         
      • dehydration.
        • 
                                                      
      
                                                   
      • 
      
                                                
    
                                                
    It found: (Inouye, 1999)
    
                                                
      
                                                   
    • There were significantly fewer first episodes of
                                                         delirium with the multicomponent strategy
                                                      vs usual care (44 [9.9%] vs 64 [15%] respectively)
      • 
                                                   
    • Matched group analysis found an odds ratio (OR) of 0.60 (95%CI:
                                                      0.39-0.92; p=0.02)
      • 
                                                   
    • Total number of days with delirium was
                                                      significantly lower with the multicomponent
                                                         strategy vs usual care (105 vs. 161 days respectively,
                                                      p=0.02)
      • 
                                                
    
                                                
    Delirium severity and recurrence rates were not
                                                      significantly different in the 2 groups.
    
                                             
    
                                          
    
                                       
    
      
                                    
    
      
                                 
    
                                 
                              
    
                              
    
                                 
    
                                    
    
                                       
                                          
                                             
                                                
                                                   Plus
                                                    – 
                                                
                                                
                                                   
                                                   Set an escalation plan
                                                
                                                
                                                   
                                                
                                             
                                          
                                       
                                    
    
                                 
    
                                 
    
      
                                    
    
                                       
                                       
                                       
    
      
                                       
    Set an escalation plan for all patients with dementia as early as
                                             possible.
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • This should include: (Fritz Z, et al, 2017):
                                             
        
                                                
      • Resuscitation status (i.e., do not attempt
                                                   cardiopulmonary resuscitation [DNACPR] decision)
        • 
                                                
      • Ceiling of care (e.g., is the patient suitable for
                                                   intubation or intensive care admission)
        • 
                                             
      
                                          
      • 
      
                                          
    • Escalation plans should take account of advanced care planning,
                                             including legally-binding advanced directives. (Fritz Z, et al, 2017)
                                          
      • 
                                       
    
                                       
    Communicate all decisions regarding the escalation plan
                                          with the patient.(Fritz Z, et al, 2017)
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • Assess mental capacity (the ability to make decisions at a
                                             specific time a decision needs to be made) and document. (NICE, 2018)
                                             Follow the appropriate legislation in your region.
      • 
                                          
    • In England and Wales, health professionals must comply with the 2005
                                                Mental Capacity Act. (Department of Health, 2005)
                                             Assessments should follow the principles in the Act. (Department of
                                                Health, 2005) 
      • 
                                          
    • If a patient is assessed to lack mental capacity, ensure decisions are made in
                                             the best interests of the patient. (Department of Health,
                                                2005) (NICE, 2018)
      • 
                                       
    
                                       
    If the patient is assessed to lack mental capacity, consult with the
                                             patient’s next of kin in order to make ‘best interests’
                                             decisions. (Department of Health, 2005) 
    
                                       
    According to the 2005 Mental Capacity Act in England and Wales, if a patient is
                                          unbefriended and a decision is not time critical, an independent mental capacity
                                          advocate (IMCA) should be sought to perform this role instead. (Social Care
                                             Institute for Excellence, 2011)
    
      
                                    
    
      
                                 
    
                                 
                              
    
                              
    
                                 
    
                                    
    
                                       
                                          
                                             
                                                
                                                   Plus
                                                    – 
                                                
                                                
                                                   
                                                   Remember to prescribe usual inhalers
                                                
                                                
                                                   
                                                
                                             
                                          
                                       
                                    
    
                                 
    
                                 
    
      
                                    
    
                                       
                                       
                                       
    
      
                                       
    Patients may not think to tell you about their usual inhalers when asked
                                             about their medication, so remember to check and prescribe these if
                                             appropriate
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • Patients with asthma should continue any inhaled corticosteroid medication they
                                             usually take. These should not be stopped without a clear medical reason.
      • 
                                          
    • Many inhalers are combined medications, so check there is no dual prescribing
                                          
      • 
                                       
    
                                       
    Temporarily stop inhaled long-acting muscarinic receptor antagonists if your
                                             patient with COPD or asthma usually takes this and develops acute kidney injury
                                             with an eGFR <30.
    
      
      
      
                                    
    
      
                                 
    
                                 
                              
    
                              
    
                                 
    
                                    
    
                                       
                                          
                                             
                                                
                                                   Consider
                                                    – 
                                                
                                                
                                                   
                                                   Nicotene replacement therapy in current
                                                      smokers
                                                
                                                
                                                   
                                                
                                             
                                          
                                       
                                    
    
                                 
    
                                 
    
      
                                    
    
                                       
                                       
                                       
    
      
                                       
    All current smokers are nicotine dependent
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • Nicotine replacement therapy should be prescribed to prevent withdrawal
                                             regardless of a patient’s intention regarding smoking cessation
      • 
                                          
    • Please consult your local hospital guidance.
      • 
                                       
    
                                       

    Make every contact count:
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • Being in hospital with an acute illness is the ideal time to support someone to
                                             give up smoking
      • 
                                          
    • Provide brief advice.
      • 
                                       
    
      
      
      
                                    
    
      
                                 
    
                                 
                              
    
                              
    
                                 
    
                                    
    
                                       
                                          
                                             
                                                
                                                   Plus
                                                    – 
                                                
                                                
                                                   
                                                   Monitor for and manage any acute exacerbation of
                                                      asthma or COPD
                                                
                                                
                                                   
                                                
                                             
                                          
                                       
                                    
    
                                 
    
                                 
    
      
                                    
    
                                       
                                       
                                       
    
      
                                       
    Monitor patient for symptoms and signs of an acute exacerbation of their
                                             COPD or asthma.
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • A patient with airways disease may have an acute exacerbation while in hospital
                                             with a separate another medical or surgical condition.
      • 
                                       
    
                                       
    Patients with comorbid COPD may be having an acute exacerbation if there is
                                             an acute worsening of respiratory symptoms that results in the need for
                                             additional therapy.(Global Initiative for Chronic Obstructive Lung
                                             Disease, 2019)
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • Differentiate from other conditions which may present very
                                             similarly, such as acute coronary syndrome, acute heart
                                                failure and pneumonia.
      • 
                                          
    • Follow guideline recommendations on investigations, including:
                                             
        
                                                
      • ABG measurement
        • 
                                                
      • Chest x-ray. Generally, do a chest x-ray if a patient
                                                   with COPD is admitted with breathlessness or cough, or has worse symptoms
                                                   than usual.
        • 
                                             
      
                                          
      • 
      
                                          
    • Follow COPD guideline recommendations on initial
                                                management, including:
        
                                                
      • Assessing severity
        • 
                                                
      • Increasing dose and/or frequency of bronchodilator
                                                      therapy, using nebulisers (driven with air) or spacers
        • 
                                                
      • Considering oral corticosteroids,
                                                   antibiotics and need for noninvasive
                                                      ventilation.
        • 
                                                
      • See BMJ Best Practice topic on Acute exacerbation in COPD
        • 
                                             
      
                                          
      • 
      
                                       
    
                                       
    Follow guideline recommendations for assessment of severity and management
                                             of an acute exacerbation of asthma in adults. (British
                                             Thoracic Society; Scottish Intercollegiate Guidelines Network, 2016) 
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • Initial treatment of acute severe asthma includes:
        
                                                
      • Supplementary oxygen if hypoxaemic. Titrate to maintain
                                                   an oxygen saturation level of 94% to 98%
        • 
                                                
      • High-dose inhaled beta2 agonists as early as possible,
                                                   usually via nebuliser (oxygen-driven)
        • 
                                                
      • Ipratropium bromide via nebuliser (oxygen-driven) if
                                                   needed
        • 
                                                
      • Corticosteroids in adequate doses (40 to 50 mg daily for
                                                   at least 5 days)
        • 
                                                
      • Other therapies may be required, follow guideline
                                                      recommendations
        • 
                                                
      • See BMJ Best Practice topic on Acute exacerbation of asthma in adults.
                                                
        • 
                                             
      
                                          
      • 
      
                                       
    
                                       
    Stop long-acting muscarinic receptor antagonists (LAMAs) e.g., tiotropium
                                             temporarily if short-acting muscarinic antagonists (SAMAs) e.g., ipratropium
                                             bromide are prescribed via nebuliser.
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • This is based on expert opinion due to concern over possible anticholinergic
                                             adverse effects.
      • 
                                       
    
                                       
    Regular nebulisers should only be prescribed for 24 to 48 hours and then
                                             switched back to the patient’s usual inhalers.
    
      
      
                                       
    
                                          
    
                                             
                                             
                                             
    
                                                
    Beta2-agonists increase the risk of myocardial infarction and other
                                                      adverse cardiovascular events
    
                                                
    Beta2-agonists increase heart rate and reduce potassium levels.
                                                   (Salpeter SR, et al, 2004) They are absorbed into the systemic
                                                   circulation and there is evidence they are associated with an increased
                                                   risk of myocardial infarction, unable angina, congestive heart failure and
                                                   arrhythmias. (Au DH, et al, 2002) (Salpeter SR, et al, 2004) (Baker JG,
                                                      2017)
    
                                             
    
                                          
    
                                       
    
                                       
    
                                          
    
                                             
                                             
                                             
    
                                                
    A systematic review found no difference in outcomes (treatment
                                                      failure, time to next exacerbation, time in hospital, lung function,
                                                      adverse effects, mortality) between short courses (7 days or less) vs
                                                      more conventional longer courses (longer than 7 days) of corticosteroids
                                                      in people with acute exacerbation of COPD (Walters JAE, et
                                                      al, 2018)
    
                                                
      
                                                   
    • The randomised controlled trials included in this Cochrane review were
                                                      all in hospital settings and only included people with severe to very
                                                      severe COPD (Walters JAE, et al, 2018)
      • 
                                                   
    • The review authors concluded that due to the addition of a new trial,
                                                      they have increased confidence that a corticosteroid course of around 5
                                                      days is likely to be sufficient for the treatment of an acute
                                                      exacerbation of COPD. (Walters JAE, et al, 2018)
      • 
                                                
    
                                             
    
                                          
    
                                       
    
      
                                    
    
      
                                 
    
                                 
                              
    
                              
    
                                 
    
                                    
    
                                       
                                          
                                             
                                                
                                                   Plus
                                                    – 
                                                
                                                
                                                   
                                                   Check patient’s feet
                                                
                                                
                                                   
                                                
                                             
                                          
                                       
                                    
    
                                 
    
                                 
    
      
                                    
    
                                       
                                       
                                       
    
      
                                       
    Check the feet of all adults with diabetes on admission to hospital and
                                             whenever there has been a change in their status. (NICE, 2016)
                                       
    
                                       
      
                                          
    • Why? 
                                             
        
                                                
      • To detect new ulceration or infection,
                                                   which may be unnoticed by the patient andmay even
                                                   be the underlying cause of their illness (e.g., patient
                                                   presenting with sepsis, spinal abscess or endocarditis where the original
                                                   focus of infection is the foot lesion).
        • 
                                                
      • To determine if patient is at high risk of pressure ulceration
                                                   and requires pressure relief.
        • 
                                             
      
                                          
      • 
      
                                          
    • Take action 
                                             
        
                                                
      • You must remove any foot dressings to see if any
                                                   wound/ulcer is infected. (NICE NG19, 2016)
        • 
                                                
      • Inspect the foot for lesions and examine for loss of
                                                   protective sensation. Follow your local guidelines, but a
                                                   quick simple test is the Ipswich Touch Test©️.
                                                   (Rayman G, et al, 2011)
        • 
                                                
      • If your patient has reduced sensation, they are at
                                                   high risk of pressure ulceration. Inform the nursing staff
                                                   and provide pressure relieving devices.
        • 
                                                
      • A daily heel check for signs of pressure
                                                      trauma should be undertaken by nursing or health care assistant
                                                   staff.
        • 
                                             
      
                                          
      • 
                                       
    
      
                                    
    
      
                                 
    
                                 
                              
    

                           
    
                        
    
                     
    

                     
    
                  
    

                  
    
                     
    
                        
    
                                                            
                              Haemodynamically stable: PE confirmed (on CTPA) or highly suspected (Wells>4 or positive D-dimer)
                           

                           
                              
                                 VIEW ALL
                                 
                              
                           

                        
    
                     
    
                     
    

                        
    
                           
    
                              
    
                                                                     
                                    
                                       
                                       
                                          No contraindication to anticoagulation
                                           – 
                                       
                                    
                                 
                              
    
                           
    
                           
    
                              
    
                                 
    
                                    
    
                                                                           
                                          
                                             
                                                
                                                   1st line
                                                    – 
                                                
                                                
                                                   
                                                   Anticoagulation
                                                
                                             
                                          
                                       
                                    
    
                                 
    
                                 
    
      
                                    
    Base your choice of anticoagulant on the patient’s comorbidities and
                                          contraindications as well as taking account of local guidelines. [NICE]
    
                                    
    Start initial anticoagulation as soon as possible with any one of the
                                          following: LMWH, fondaparinux, apixaban, or rivaroxaban.
    
                                    
      
                                       
    • If using LMWH or fondaparinux, continue treatment for at least 5
                                             days.[NICE][ESC]
                                          
        
                                             
      • If ongoing anticoagulation will be with warfarin, start warfarin within 24
                                                hours of diagnosis and ensure overlap with LMWH or fondaparinux for at least 5
                                                days or until the INR is ≥2 for at least 24 hours (whichever is the
                                                longer).[NICE] 
        OR
        
                                             
        • 
                                             
      • If ongoing anticoagulation will be with edoxaban or dabigatran, at least 5
                                                days of lead-in therapy with LMWH or fondaparinux is required first. Stop the
                                                LMWH or fondaparinux before starting dabigatran or edoxaban.[BTS]
        • 
                                          
      
                                       
      • 
                                       
    • If using rivaroxaban or apixaban, these drugs may be started without the
                                             need for lead-in therapy with a parenteral anticoagulant first.
                                          
        
                                             
      • Acute-phase treatment consists of an increased dose of the oral anticoagulant
                                                over the first 3 weeks (for rivaroxaban), or over the first 7 days (for
                                                apixaban).
        • 
                                             
      • The 2018 British Thoracic Society (BTS) guideline recommends using a single
                                                direct-acting oral anticoagulant (DOAC) in patients being considered for
                                                outpatient management to minimise potential confusion over dosing and
                                                administration. [BTS]
        • 
                                          
      
                                       
      • 
                                    
    
      
                                    
    
                                       
    
                                          
                                          
    Dabigatran and edoxaban are not recommended as the initial
                                             anticoagulant because they require lead-in therapy with a parenteral
                                             anticoagulant first.
    
                                       
    
                                    
    
      
                                    
    
                                       
    
                                          
                                          
    Never give a DOAC simultaneously with parenteral
                                             anticoagulation
    
                                          
      
                                             
    • While warfarin is started at the same time as a parenteral anticoagulant and
                                                overlapped for at least 5 days or until the INR is ≥2 for at least 24 hours
                                                (whichever is the longer), DOACs should never be overlapped or given at the
                                                same time as a parenteral anticoagulant.
      • 
                                             
    • Apixaban and rivaroxaban may be started without the need for lead-in therapy
                                                with a parenteral anticoagulant first. Either of these DOACs can be used as a
                                                single-drug approach; this is why BTS guidelines recommend them as the
                                                preferred DOAC options in any patient who might be suitable for early
                                                discharge. [BTS]
      • 
                                             
    • However, dabigatran and edoxaban require at least 5 days lead-in therapy with
                                                a parenteral anticoagulant before starting treatment. The parenteral
                                                anticoagulant should be stopped before dabigatran or edoxaban are started.
                                             
      • 
                                          
    
                                       
    
                                    
    
                                    
    DOACs have non-inferior efficacy and are possibly safer,
                                       particularly in terms of major bleeding, than the standard regimen of LMWH plus
                                          warfarin.[ESC] [BTS] [ADD CCA link]
    
                                    
      
                                       
    • Select a DOAC after discussion with the patient about which regimen would be most
                                          suited to them as well as taking into account the risks and benefits of each DOAC.
                                       
      • 
                                    
    
      
      
                                    
    
                                       
    
                                          
                                          
                                          
    
                                             
    There is now strong evidence that DOACs are non-inferior and have a
                                                   favourable safety profile for management of PE when compared to either
                                                   LMWH/warfarin or fondaparinux. [BTS]
    
                                             
      
                                                
    • Results of the major trials using DOACs in the treatment of VTE indicate
                                                   that these agents are non-inferior (in terms of efficacy) and possibly
                                                   safer (particularly in terms of major bleeding) than the standard
                                                   heparin/warfarin regimen. [WELLS][ESC][GALLUS] [BRENNER] [COCHRANE] [LIB]
                                                
      • 
                                                
    • A Cochrane review concluded there was [CCA LINK AS ABOVE]
      • 
                                                
        
                                                   
      • no difference between dabigatran and the standard regimen in preventing
                                                      recurrent VTE (OR 1.02, 95% CI 0.50 to 2.04), recurrent VTE (OR 0.93,
                                                      95% CI 0.52 to 1.66) and DVT (OR 0.79, 95% CI 0.29 to 2.13) or in
                                                      causing major bleeding (OR 0.50, 95% CI 0.15 to 1.68)
        • 
                                                   
      • no difference between the factor Xa inhibitors (apixaban, rivaroxaban,
                                                      edoxaban) compared to the standard regimen, in preventing recurrent VTE
                                                      (OR 0.85, 95% CI 0.15 to 1.68), DVT (OR 0.72, 95% CI 0.39 to 1.32) and
                                                      all-cause mortality (OR 1.16, 95% CI 0.59 to 1.62) or in causing major
                                                      bleeding (OR 0.97, 95% CI 0.59 to 1.62).
        • 
                                                
      
                                                
    • High time in therapeutic range (TTR) values were achieved under warfarin
                                                   treatment in all trials. On the other hand, the study populations included
                                                   relatively young patients, very few of whom had cancer.
      • 
                                             
    
                                             
    Experience with DOACs remains limited but they can be viewed as an
